</tr><tr><td class="hlist" style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.2em;">

<p>The <b>Tokugawa shogunate</b>, also known as the <b>Tokugawa Bakufu</b><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">徳川幕府</span>)</span> and the <b>Edo Bakufu</b><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">江戸幕府</span>)</span>, was the last <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a> <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a> <a href="/wiki/Shogunate" class="mw-redirect" title="Shogunate">military government</a>, which existed between 1600 and 1868.<sup id="cite_ref-nussbaum978_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nussbaum978-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The head of government was the <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dgun" title="Shōgun">shogun</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and each was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_clan" title="Tokugawa clan">Tokugawa clan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nussbaum976_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nussbaum976-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The Tokugawa shogunate ruled from <a href="/wiki/Edo_Castle" title="Edo Castle">Edo Castle</a> and the years of the shogunate became known as the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This time is also called the Tokugawa period<sup id="cite_ref-nussbaum978_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nussbaum978-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> or pre-modern (<i>Kinsei</i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">近世</span>)</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bakumatsu" title="Bakumatsu">Bakumatsu</a></div>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Sengoku_period" title="Sengoku period">Sengoku period</a> ("warring states period"), the central government had been largely re-established by <a href="/wiki/Oda_Nobunaga" title="Oda Nobunaga">Oda Nobunaga</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Azuchi%E2%80%93Momoyama_period" title="Azuchi–Momoyama period">Azuchi–Momoyama period</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sekigahara" title="Battle of Sekigahara">Battle of Sekigahara</a> in 1600, central authority fell to <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Ieyasu" title="Tokugawa Ieyasu">Tokugawa Ieyasu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nussbaum978_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nussbaum978-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Edo_society" title="Edo society">Society in the Tokugawa period</a>, unlike in previous shogunates, was supposedly based on the strict class hierarchy originally established by <a href="/wiki/Toyotomi_Hideyoshi" title="Toyotomi Hideyoshi">Toyotomi Hideyoshi</a>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Daimy%C5%8D" title="Daimyō">daimyō</a></i> (lords) were at the top, followed by the warrior-caste of <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a>, with the farmers, artisans, and traders ranking below. In some parts of the country, particularly smaller regions, <i>daimyō</i> and samurai were more or less identical, since <i>daimyō</i> might be trained as samurai, and samurai might act as local rulers. Otherwise, the largely inflexible nature of this social stratification system unleashed disruptive forces over time. Taxes on the peasantry were set at fixed amounts that did not account for inflation or other changes in monetary value. As a result, the tax revenues collected by the samurai landowners were worth less and less over time. This often led to numerous confrontations between noble but impoverished samurai and well-to-do peasants, ranging from simple local disturbances to much larger rebellions. None, however, proved compelling enough to seriously challenge the established order until the arrival of foreign powers.
</p><p>A 2017 study found that peasant rebellions and collective desertion ("flight") lowered tax rates and inhibited state growth in the Tokugawa shogunate.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the mid-19th century, an alliance of several of the more powerful <i>daimyō</i>, along with the titular <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor</a>, succeeded in overthrowing the shogunate after the <a href="/wiki/Boshin_War" title="Boshin War">Boshin War</a>, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a>. The Tokugawa shogunate came to an official end in 1868 with the resignation of the 15th Tokugawa shogun<i>,</i> <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Yoshinobu" title="Tokugawa Yoshinobu">Tokugawa Yoshinobu</a>, leading to the "restoration" (<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E7%8E%8B%E6%94%BF%E5%BE%A9%E5%8F%A4_(%E6%97%A5%E6%9C%AC)" class="extiw" title="ja:王政復古 (日本)">王政復古</a>, <i>Ōsei fukko</i>) of imperial rule. Notwithstanding its eventual overthrow in favor of the more modernized, less feudal form of governance of the Meiji Restoration, the Tokugawa shogunate oversaw the longest period of peace and stability in Japan's history, lasting well over 260 years.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shogunate_and_domains">Shogunate and domains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Shogunate and domains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>bakuhan taisei</i> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">幕藩体制</span>) was the <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a> political system in the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. <span id="bakufu"></span><i>Baku</i> is an abbreviation of <i>bakufu</i>, meaning "military government"—that is, the shogunate. The <i><a href="/wiki/Han_(Japan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Han (Japan)">han</a></i> were the domains headed by <i>daimyō</i>.
</p><p>Vassals held inherited lands and provided military service and homage to their lords. The <i>bakuhan taisei</i> split feudal power between the shogunate in Edo and provincial domains throughout Japan. Provinces had a degree of sovereignty and were allowed an independent administration of the han in exchange for loyalty to the <i>shōgun</i>, who was responsible for foreign relations and national security. The <i>shōgun</i> and lords were all <i>daimyōs</i>: feudal lords with their own bureaucracies, policies, and territories. The <i>shōgun</i> also administered the most powerful <i>han</i>, the hereditary fief of the House of Tokugawa. Each level of government administered its own system of <a href="/wiki/Taxation" class="mw-redirect" title="Taxation">taxation</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edo_P2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Edo_P2.jpg/220px-Edo_P2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Edo_P2.jpg/330px-Edo_P2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Edo_P2.jpg/440px-Edo_P2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2671" data-file-height="1952" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edo_P2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edo_Castle" title="Edo Castle">Edo Castle</a>, 17th century</div></div></div>
<p>The emperor, nominally a religious leader, held no real power; this was vested in the <i>shōgun</i>. The shogunate had the power to discard, annex, and transform domains. The <i><a href="/wiki/Sankin-k%C5%8Dtai" title="Sankin-kōtai">sankin-kōtai</a></i> system of alternative residence required each <i>daimyō</i> to reside in alternate years between the han and the court in Edo. During their absences from Edo, it was also required that they leave family as hostages until their return. The huge expenditure <i>sankin-kōtai</i> imposed on each han helped centralize aristocratic alliances and ensured loyalty to the <i>shōgun</i> as each representative doubled as a potential hostage.
</p><p>Tokugawa's descendants further ensured loyalty by maintaining a dogmatic insistence on loyalty to the <i>shōgun</i>. <i><a href="/wiki/Fudai_daimy%C5%8D" title="Fudai daimyō">Fudai daimyō</a></i> were hereditary vassals of Ieyasu, as well as of his descendants. <i><a href="/wiki/Tozama_daimy%C5%8D" title="Tozama daimyō">Tozama</a></i> ("outsiders") became vassals of Ieyasu after the Battle of Sekigahara. <i><a href="/wiki/Shinpan" class="mw-redirect" title="Shinpan">Shinpan</a></i> ("relatives") were collaterals of Tokugawa Hidetada. Early in the Edo period, the shogunate viewed the <i>tozama</i> as the least likely to be loyal; over time, strategic marriages and the entrenchment of the system made the <i>tozama</i> less likely to rebel. In the end, it was the great <i>tozama</i> of <a href="/wiki/Satsuma_Province" title="Satsuma Province">Satsuma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dsh%C5%AB_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Chōshū Province">Chōshū</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tosa_Domain" title="Tosa Domain">Tosa</a>, and to a lesser extent <a href="/wiki/Hizen" class="mw-redirect" title="Hizen">Hizen</a>, that brought down the shogunate. These four states are called the Four Western Clans, or <a href="/w/index.php?title=Satchotohi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Satchotohi (page does not exist)">Satchotohi</a> for short.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The number of <i>han</i> (roughly 250) fluctuated throughout the Edo period. They were ranked by size, which was measured as the number of <i><a href="/wiki/Koku" title="Koku">koku</a></i> of rice that the domain produced each year. One <i>koku</i> was the amount of rice necessary to feed one adult male for one year. The minimum number for a <i>daimyō</i> was ten thousand <i>koku</i>; the largest, apart from the <i>shōgun</i>, was a million.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_the_Emperor">Relations with the Emperor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Relations with the Emperor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edo_social_structure.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Edo_social_structure.svg/220px-Edo_social_structure.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Edo_social_structure.svg/330px-Edo_social_structure.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Edo_social_structure.svg/440px-Edo_social_structure.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="842" data-file-height="595" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edo_social_structure.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Social class during the Shogunate with the Emperor as the nominal ruler</div></div></div>
<p>Regardless of the political title of the Emperor, the <i>shōguns</i> of the Tokugawa family controlled Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The administration<span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">体制</span><span style="display:none">,</span>&#32;<i>taisei</i>)</span> of Japan was a task given by the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Court_in_Kyoto" title="Imperial Court in Kyoto">Imperial Court in Kyoto</a> to the Tokugawa family, which returned to the court in the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a>. While the Emperor officially had the prerogative of appointing the <i>shōgun</i>, he had virtually no say in state affairs. The shogunate appointed a liaison, the <i><a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Shoshidai" title="Kyoto Shoshidai">Kyoto Shoshidai</a></i> (<i>Shogun's Representative in Kyoto</i>), to deal with the Emperor, court and nobility.
</p><p>Towards the end of the shogunate, however, after centuries of the Emperor having very little say in state affairs and being secluded in his <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Imperial_Palace" title="Kyoto Imperial Palace">Kyoto palace</a>, and in the wake of the reigning <i>shōgun</i>, <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Iemochi" title="Tokugawa Iemochi">Tokugawa Iemochi</a>, marrying the sister of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_K%C5%8Dmei" title="Emperor Kōmei">Emperor Kōmei</a> (r. 1846–1867), in 1862, the Imperial Court in Kyoto began to enjoy increased political influence.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The Emperor would occasionally be consulted on various policies and the shogun even made a visit to Kyoto to visit the Emperor.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shogun_and_foreign_trade">Shogun and foreign trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Shogun and foreign trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grote_partij_bij_het_opperhoofd_van_Dejima.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Grote_partij_bij_het_opperhoofd_van_Dejima.jpg/220px-Grote_partij_bij_het_opperhoofd_van_Dejima.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Grote_partij_bij_het_opperhoofd_van_Dejima.jpg/330px-Grote_partij_bij_het_opperhoofd_van_Dejima.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Grote_partij_bij_het_opperhoofd_van_Dejima.jpg/440px-Grote_partij_bij_het_opperhoofd_van_Dejima.jpg 2x" data-file-width="731" data-file-height="562" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grote_partij_bij_het_opperhoofd_van_Dejima.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dutch trading post in <a href="/wiki/Dejima" title="Dejima">Dejima</a>, c. 1805</div></div></div>
<p>Foreign affairs and trade were monopolized by the <i>shogunate</i>, yielding a huge profit. Foreign trade was also permitted to the <a href="/wiki/Satsuma_Domain" title="Satsuma Domain">Satsuma</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tsushima_Fuch%C5%AB_Domain" class="mw-redirect" title="Tsushima Fuchū Domain">Tsushima domains</a>. <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">Rice</a> was the main trading product of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> during this time. <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">Isolationism</a> was the foreign policy of Japan and trade was strictly controlled.  Merchants were outsiders to the <a href="/wiki/Social_hierarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Social hierarchy">social hierarchy</a> of Japan and were thought to be greedy.
</p><p>The visits of the <a href="/wiki/Nanban_trade" title="Nanban trade">Nanban</a> ships from <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> were at first the main vector of trade exchanges, followed by the addition of Dutch, English and sometimes Spanish ships.
</p><p>From 1603 onward, Japan started to participate actively in foreign trade. In 1615, an embassy and trade mission under <a href="/wiki/Hasekura_Tsunenaga" title="Hasekura Tsunenaga">Hasekura Tsunenaga</a> was sent across the Pacific to <a href="/wiki/Nueva_Espa%C3%B1a" class="mw-redirect" title="Nueva España">Nueva España</a> (New Spain) on the Japanese-built galleon <a href="/wiki/Japanese_warship_San_Juan_Bautista" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese warship San Juan Bautista"><i>San Juan Bautista</i></a>. Until 1635, the Shogun issued numerous permits for the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Red_seal_ships" title="Red seal ships">red seal ships</a>" destined for the Asian trade.
</p><p>After 1635 and the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">Seclusion laws</a>, inbound ships were only allowed from <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shogun_and_Christianity">Shogun and Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Shogun and Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kirishitan" title="Kirishitan">Kirishitan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Christian_prisoners.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Christian_prisoners.png/220px-Christian_prisoners.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Christian_prisoners.png/330px-Christian_prisoners.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Christian_prisoners.png/440px-Christian_prisoners.png 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="471" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Christian_prisoners.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Christian prisoners in Edo, 17th century</div></div></div>
<p>Followers of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> first began appearing in Japan during the 16th century. <a href="/wiki/Oda_Nobunaga" title="Oda Nobunaga">Oda Nobunaga</a> embraced Christianity and the Western technology that was imported with it, such as the musket. He also saw it as a tool he could use to suppress Buddhist forces.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Though Christianity was allowed to grow until the 1610s, <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Ieyasu" title="Tokugawa Ieyasu">Tokugawa Ieyasu</a> soon began to see it as a growing threat to the stability of the shogunate. As <i>Ōgosho</i> ("Cloistered <i>Shōgun</i>"),<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> he influenced the implementation of laws that banned the practice of Christianity. His successors followed suit, compounding upon Ieyasu's laws. The ban of Christianity is often linked with the creation of the Seclusion laws, or <a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">Sakoku</a>, in the 1630s.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutions_of_the_shogunate">Institutions of the shogunate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Institutions of the shogunate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="R.C5.8Dj.C5.AB_and_wakadoshiyori"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rōjū_and_wakadoshiyori">Rōjū and wakadoshiyori</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rōjū and wakadoshiyori">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/R%C5%8Dj%C5%AB" title="Rōjū">rōjū</a></i> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">老中</span>) were the senior members of the shogunate. They supervised the <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Cmetsuke" class="mw-redirect" title="Ōmetsuke">ōmetsuke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Machi-bugy%C5%8D" title="Machi-bugyō">machi-bugyō</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ongokubugy%C5%8D&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ongokubugyō (page does not exist)">ongokubugyō</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E9%81%A0%E5%9B%BD%E5%A5%89%E8%A1%8C" class="extiw" title="ja:遠国奉行">ja</a>&#93;</span> (遠国奉行) and other officials, oversaw relations with the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Court_in_Kyoto" title="Imperial Court in Kyoto">Imperial Court in Kyoto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuge" title="Kuge">kuge</a> (members of the nobility), daimyō, Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Temple" title="Temple">temples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">Shinto shrines</a>, and attended to matters like divisions of <a href="/wiki/Fief" title="Fief">fiefs</a>. Normally, four or five men held the office, and one was on duty for a month at a time on a rotating basis. They conferred on especially important matters. In the administrative reforms of 1867 (<a href="/wiki/Kei%C5%8D_Reforms" title="Keiō Reforms">Keiō Reforms</a>), the office was eliminated in favor of a bureaucratic system with ministers for the interior, finance, foreign relations, army, and navy.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SakuradaGate2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/SakuradaGate2.jpg/220px-SakuradaGate2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/SakuradaGate2.jpg/330px-SakuradaGate2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/SakuradaGate2.jpg/440px-SakuradaGate2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="407" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SakuradaGate2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sakuradamon Gate of <a href="/wiki/Edo_Castle" title="Edo Castle">Edo Castle</a> where <a href="/wiki/Ii_Naosuke" title="Ii Naosuke">Ii Naosuke</a> was assassinated in 1860</div></div></div>
<p>In principle, the requirements for appointment to the office of rōjū were to be a <i><a href="/wiki/Fudai_daimy%C5%8D" title="Fudai daimyō">fudai daimyō</a></i> and to have a fief assessed at <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7004500000000000000♠"></span>50<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span></span> <i><a href="/wiki/Koku" title="Koku">koku</a></i> or more. However, there were exceptions to both criteria. Many appointees came from the offices close to the <i>shōgun</i>, such as <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soba_y%C5%8Dnin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Soba yōnin (page does not exist)">soba yōnin</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%81%B4%E7%94%A8%E4%BA%BA" class="extiw" title="ja:側用人">ja</a>&#93;</span></i> (側用人), <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Shoshidai" title="Kyoto Shoshidai">Kyoto Shoshidai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Osaka_j%C5%8Ddai" title="Osaka jōdai">Osaka jōdai</a>.
</p><p>Irregularly, the <i>shōguns</i> appointed a <i>rōjū</i> to the position of <i><a href="/wiki/Tair%C5%8D" title="Tairō">tairō</a></i> (great elder). The office was limited to members of the <a href="/wiki/Ii_clan" title="Ii clan">Ii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sakai_clan" title="Sakai clan">Sakai</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Doi_clan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Doi clan (page does not exist)">Doi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hotta_clan" title="Hotta clan">Hotta clans</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Yanagisawa_Yoshiyasu" title="Yanagisawa Yoshiyasu">Yanagisawa Yoshiyasu</a> was given the status of tairō as well. Among the most famous was <a href="/wiki/Ii_Naosuke" title="Ii Naosuke">Ii Naosuke</a>, who was assassinated in 1860 outside the Sakuradamon Gate of <a href="/wiki/Edo_Castle" title="Edo Castle">Edo Castle</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sakuradamon_Incident_(1860)" title="Sakuradamon Incident (1860)">Sakuradamon incident</a>).
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Wakadoshiyori" title="Wakadoshiyori">wakadoshiyori</a></i> were next in status below the rōjū. An outgrowth of the early six-man <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rokuninsh%C5%AB&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Rokuninshū (page does not exist)">rokuninshū</a></i> (六人衆, 1633–1649), the office took its name and final form in 1662, but with four members. Their primary responsibility was management of the affairs of the <a href="/wiki/Hatamoto" title="Hatamoto">hatamoto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gokenin" title="Gokenin">gokenin</a>, the direct vassals of the <i>shōgun</i>.
</p><p>Some <i>shōguns</i> appointed a <i>soba yōnin</i>. This person acted as a liaison between the <i>shōgun</i> and the <i>rōjū</i>. The <i>soba yōnin</i> increased in importance during the time of the fifth <i>shōgun</i> <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Tsunayoshi" title="Tokugawa Tsunayoshi">Tokugawa Tsunayoshi</a>, when a wakadoshiyori, <a href="/wiki/Inaba_Masayasu" title="Inaba Masayasu">Inaba Masayasu</a>, assassinated <a href="/wiki/Hotta_Masatoshi" title="Hotta Masatoshi">Hotta Masatoshi</a>, the <i>tairō</i>. Fearing for his personal safety, Tsunayoshi moved the <i>rōjū</i> to a more distant part of the castle. Some of the most famous <i>soba yōnin</i> were <a href="/wiki/Yanagisawa_Yoshiyasu" title="Yanagisawa Yoshiyasu">Yanagisawa Yoshiyasu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanuma_Okitsugu" title="Tanuma Okitsugu">Tanuma Okitsugu</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id=".C5.8Cmetsuke_and_metsuke"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ōmetsuke_and_metsuke">Ōmetsuke and metsuke</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ōmetsuke and metsuke">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>ōmetsuke</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Metsuke" title="Metsuke">metsuke</a></i> were officials who reported to the <i>rōjū</i> and <i>wakadoshiyori</i>. The five <i>ōmetsuke</i> were in charge of monitoring the affairs of the <i>daimyōs</i>, <i>kuge</i> and imperial court. They were in charge of discovering any threat of rebellion. Early in the Edo period, <i>daimyōs</i> such as <a href="/wiki/Yagy%C5%AB_Munefuyu" title="Yagyū Munefuyu">Yagyū Munefuyu</a> held the office. Soon, however, it fell to <i><a href="/wiki/Hatamoto" title="Hatamoto">hatamoto</a></i> with rankings of 5,000 <i>koku</i> or more. To give them authority in their dealings with <i>daimyōs</i>, they were often ranked at 10,000 <i>koku</i> and given the title of <i><a href="/wiki/Kokushi_(official)" title="Kokushi (official)">kami</a></i> (an ancient title, typically signifying the governor of a <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Japan" title="Provinces of Japan">province</a>) such as <i>Bizen-no-kami</i>.
</p><p>As time progressed, the function of the <i>ōmetsuke</i> evolved into one of passing orders from the shogunate to the <i>daimyōs</i>, and of administering to ceremonies within Edo Castle. They also took on additional responsibilities such as supervising religious affairs and controlling firearms. The <i>metsuke</i>, reporting to the <i>wakadoshiyori</i>, oversaw the affairs of the vassals of the <i>shōgun</i>. They were the police force for the thousands of hatamoto and <a href="/wiki/Gokenin" title="Gokenin">gokenin</a> who were concentrated in Edo. Individual han had their own <i>metsuke</i> who similarly policed their samurai.
</p>
<h3><span id="San-bugy.C5.8D"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="San-bugyō">San-bugyō</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: San-bugyō">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>san-<a href="/wiki/Bugy%C5%8D" title="Bugyō">bugyō</a></i> ("three administrators") were the <i>jisha</i>, <i>kanjō</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Machi-bugy%C5%8D" title="Machi-bugyō">machi-bugyō</a></i>, which oversaw <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_temples_in_Japan" title="Buddhist temples in Japan">temples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">shrines</a>, accounting, and the cities, respectively. The <i><a href="/wiki/Jisha-bugy%C5%8D" title="Jisha-bugyō">jisha-bugyō</a></i> had the highest status of the three. They oversaw the administration of Buddhist temples (<i>ji</i>) and Shinto shrines (<i>sha</i>), many of which held fiefs. Also, they heard lawsuits from several land holdings outside the eight <a href="/wiki/Kant%C5%8D_region" title="Kantō region">Kantō</a> provinces. The appointments normally went to <i>daimyōs</i>; <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Coka_Tadasuke" title="Ōoka Tadasuke">Ōoka Tadasuke</a> was an exception, though he later became a <i>daimyō</i>.
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Kanj%C5%8D-bugy%C5%8D" title="Kanjō-bugyō">kanjō-bugyō</a></i> were next in status. The four holders of this office reported to the <i>rōjū</i>. They were responsible for the finances of the shogunate.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>machi-bugyō</i> were the chief city administrators of Edo and other cities. Their roles included mayor, chief of the police (and, later, also of the fire department), and judge in criminal and civil matters not involving samurai. Two (briefly, three) men, normally hatamoto, held the office, and alternated by month.
</p><p>Three Edo <i>machi bugyō</i> have become famous through <i><a href="/wiki/Jidaigeki" title="Jidaigeki">jidaigeki</a></i> (period films): <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Coka_Tadasuke" title="Ōoka Tadasuke">Ōoka Tadasuke</a> and <a href="/wiki/T%C5%8Dyama_Kagemoto" title="Tōyama Kagemoto">Tōyama Kagemoto</a> (Kinshirō) as heroes, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Torii_Y%C5%8Dz%C5%8D&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Torii Yōzō (page does not exist)">Torii Yōzō</a> (<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E9%B3%A5%E5%B1%85%E8%80%80%E8%94%B5" class="extiw" title="ja:鳥居耀蔵">ja:鳥居耀蔵</a>) as a villain.
</p>
<h3><span id="Tenry.C5.8D.2C_gundai_and_daikan"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenryō,_gundai_and_daikan">Tenryō, gundai and daikan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tenryō, gundai and daikan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>san-bugyō</i> together sat on a council called the <i><a href="/wiki/Hy%C5%8Dj%C5%8Dsho" title="Hyōjōsho">hyōjōsho</a></i>. In this capacity, they were responsible for administering the <i>tenryō</i>, supervising the <i>gundai</i> (<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E9%83%A1%E4%BB%A3" class="extiw" title="ja:郡代">郡代</a>), the <i>daikan</i> (<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E4%BB%A3%E5%AE%98" class="extiw" title="ja:代官">代官</a>) and the <i>kura bugyō</i> (<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E8%94%B5%E5%A5%89%E8%A1%8C" class="extiw" title="ja:蔵奉行">蔵奉行</a>), as well as hearing cases involving samurai.
</p><p>The shogun directly held lands in various parts of Japan. These were known as <i>shihaisho</i> (支配所); since the Meiji period, the term <i>tenryō</i> (<a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%A4%A9%E9%A0%98" class="extiw" title="ja:天領">天領</a>, "Emperor's land") has become synonymous.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In addition to the territory that Ieyasu held prior to the Battle of Sekigahara, this included lands he gained in that battle and lands gained as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Osaka" title="Siege of Osaka">Summer and Winter Sieges of Osaka</a>. By the end of the seventeenth century, the shogun's landholdings had reached four million koku. Such major cities as Nagasaki and Osaka, and <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mines</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Sado_Province" title="Sado Province">Sado</a> <a href="/wiki/Gold_mine" class="mw-redirect" title="Gold mine">gold mine</a>, also fell into this category.
</p>
<h3><span id="Gaikoku_bugy.C5.8D"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaikoku_bugyō">Gaikoku bugyō</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Gaikoku bugyō">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Gaikoku_bugy%C5%8D" title="Gaikoku bugyō">gaikoku bugyō</a></i> were administrators appointed between 1858 and 1868. They were charged with overseeing trade and diplomatic relations with foreign countries, and were based in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_ports" title="Treaty ports">treaty ports</a> of Nagasaki and Kanagawa (Yokohama).
</p>
<h2><span id="Late_Tokugawa_shogunate_.281853.E2.80.931867.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Tokugawa_shogunate_(1853–1867)">Late Tokugawa shogunate (1853–1867)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tokugawa_shogunate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Late Tokugawa shogunate (1853–1867)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bakumatsu" title="Bakumatsu">Bakumatsu</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Satsuma-samurai-during-boshin-war-period.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Satsuma-samurai-during-boshin-war-period.jpg/220px-Satsuma-samurai-during-boshin-war-period.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Satsuma-samurai-during-boshin-war-period.jpg/330px-Satsuma-samurai-during-boshin-war-period.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Satsuma-samurai-during-boshin-war-period.jpg/440px-Satsuma-samurai-during-boshin-war-period.jpg 2x" data-file-width="999" data-file-height="786" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Satsuma-samurai-during-boshin-war-period.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">Samurai</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Shimazu_clan" title="Shimazu clan">Shimazu clan</a></div></div></div>
<p>The late Tokugawa shogunate (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">幕末</span> <i>Bakumatsu</i>) was the period between 1853 and 1867, during which Japan ended its <a href="/wiki/Isolationist_foreign_policy" class="mw-redirect" title="Isolationist foreign policy">isolationist foreign policy</a> called <i><a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">sakoku</a></i> and modernized from a <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a> shogunate to the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Meiji period">Meiji government</a>. It is at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> and preceded the Meiji era. The major ideological and political factions during this period were divided into the pro-imperialist <i><a href="/wiki/Ishin_Shishi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ishin Shishi">Ishin Shishi</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalist">nationalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Patriotism" title="Patriotism">patriots</a>) and the shogunate forces, including the elite <a href="/wiki/Shinsengumi" title="Shinsengumi">shinsengumi</a> ("newly selected corps") swordsmen.
</p><p>Although these two groups were the most visible powers, many other factions attempted to use the chaos of the Bakumatsu era to seize personal power.<sup id="cite_ref-SHINSENGUMI_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHINSENGUMI-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, there were two other main driving forces for dissent; first, growing resentment of <i><a href="/wiki/Tozama_daimy%C5%8D" title="Tozama daimyō">tozama daimyōs</a></i>, and second, growing <a href="/wiki/Anti-Western_sentiment" title="Anti-Western sentiment">anti-Western sentiment</a> following the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Matthew_C._Perry" title="Matthew C. Perry">Matthew C. Perry</a>. The first related to those lords who had fought against Tokugawa forces at <a href="/wiki/Sekigahara" class="mw-redirect" title="Sekigahara">Sekigahara</a> (in 1600) and had from that point on been exiled permanently from all powerful positions within the shogunate. The second was to be expressed in the phrase <i><a href="/wiki/Sonn%C5%8D_j%C5%8Di" title="Sonnō jōi">sonnō jōi</a></i> ("revere the Emperor, expel the barbarians"). The end for the Bakumatsu was the <a href="/wiki/Boshin_War" title="Boshin War">Boshin War</a>, notably the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Toba%E2%80%93Fushimi" title="Battle of Toba–Fushimi">Battle of Toba–Fushimi</a>, when pro-shogunate forces were defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-LS_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LS-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">
</div></div></td></tr><tr><td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2" style="background:transparent;"><div><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">All Tokugawa <i>shōguns</i> share descent from <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Ieyasu" title="Tokugawa Ieyasu">Ieyasu</a>, who is recognized as the dynasty's founder.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p>Over the course of the Edo period, influential relatives of the shogun included:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Mitsukuni" title="Tokugawa Mitsukuni">Tokugawa Mitsukuni</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Mito_Domain" title="Mito Domain">Mito Domain</a><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Nariaki" title="Tokugawa Nariaki">Tokugawa Nariaki</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Mito_Domain" title="Mito Domain">Mito Domain</a><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Mochiharu" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokugawa Mochiharu">Tokugawa Mochiharu</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Gosanky%C5%8D" title="Gosankyō">Hitotsubashi</a> branch</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Munetake" title="Tokugawa Munetake">Tokugawa Munetake</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Gosanky%C5%8D" title="Gosankyō">Tayasu</a> branch.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Matsudaira_Katamori" title="Matsudaira Katamori">Matsudaira Katamori</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Aizu" title="Aizu">Aizu</a> branch.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Matsudaira_Sadanobu" title="Matsudaira Sadanobu">Matsudaira Sadanobu</a>, born into the <a href="/wiki/Gosanky%C5%8D" title="Gosankyō">Tayasu</a> branch, adopted into the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hisamatsu_clan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hisamatsu clan (page does not exist)">Hisamatsu-Matsudaira</a> of <a href="/wiki/Shirakawa_han" class="mw-redirect" title="Shirakawa han">Shirakawa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
